<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/templates/jsp/pb2/js/plugin/thiss-ds.js" nonce="d34d0634b6f04b9489c84cf5f63b02a7"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="d34d0634b6f04b9489c84cf5f63b02a7">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'c5281f7a-bd08-4201-9dda-510905cd5f4b'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" id="abstract-graphical-en">
         <h2 id="d8073176" class="article-section__header section__title graphical abstractlang_en graphical">Graphical Abstract</h2>
         <div class="graphical-abstract">
            <p>The functional species on the edge/plane terminals of bituminous coal are finely reconfigured by nitri-oxidation treatment to construct hard carbon with expanded carbon interlayer spacing and plentiful ultramicropores/closed pores. A large specific capacity of 356 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> is achieved along with a high Coulombic efficiency of 88.1 % and excellent rate/cycling performance.
            </p><figure>
               <div class="center graphic__image"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/203ea507-1a5d-4b40-8f9c-95b721c7384b/anie202423864-toc-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/203ea507-1a5d-4b40-8f9c-95b721c7384b/anie202423864-toc-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/05af4541-05ff-4387-8ab1-b25d641b9c74/anie202423864-toc-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/203ea507-1a5d-4b40-8f9c-95b721c7384b/anie202423864-toc-0001-m.jpg" alt="Description unavailable" title="Description unavailable" loading="lazy"></picture></a><p class="caption-style"></p>
               </div>
            </figure>
            <p></p>
            </div>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d8073179" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Hard carbons derived from coal precursors have shown bright industrial prospect as the low cost anode materials of sodium-ion batteries. However, it is of extreme necessity yet challenge to regulate carbon microstructure toward superior sodium energy storage. In this study, we propose a powerful chemical reconfiguration tactic to steer hard carbons toward high-capacity and fast sodium storage. The functional species on the edge/plane terminals of bituminous coal are finely reconfigured by nitri-oxidation treatment for effectively inhibiting the reordering of carbon layers under high temperature, thereby creating rich ultramicropores/closed pores and expanded interlayer spacing. These structural merits enable the hard carbon to garner an enhanced capacity of 356 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> along with a high initial Coulombic efficiency of 88.1 % and long lifespan. More impressively, the sodium storage kinetics is substantially sped up with a large capacity of 215 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> retained at a high-rate of 2 A g<sup>−1</sup>. This work will afford a fresh methodology for precursor modulation to accelerate the real-world practice of hard carbons toward advanced sodium-ion batteries.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>

   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202423864-sec-0001">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202423864-sec-0001-title"> Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>The global consensus on carbon neutrality necessitates the rapid transformation of energy infrastructure toward clean and sustainable energy sources from solar/wind powers.<span><sup><a href="#anie202423864-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423864-bib-0001_R_d8073168e549" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1">1</a></sup></span> In this context, the development of large-scale energy storage technology plays a crucial role to realize the holy mission.<span><sup><a href="#anie202423864-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423864-bib-0004_R_d8073168e552" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2">2</a></sup></span> Among various technology candidates, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have emerged as an attractive competitor due to their splendid advantages of rich resources in earth, cost effectiveness, and high working safety.<span><sup><a href="#anie202423864-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423864-bib-0007_R_d8073168e555" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3">3</a></sup></span> It is notable that the overall performance of SIBs is greatly reliance on the advancement of electrode materials.<span><sup><a href="#anie202423864-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423864-bib-0010_R_d8073168e558" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4">4</a></sup></span> As an indispensable component of SIBs, anode materials are of significant importance for the battery performance.<span><sup><a href="#anie202423864-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423864-bib-0013_R_d8073168e561" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5">5</a></sup></span> Carbon materials, especially hard carbons (HCs), have garnered intense scientific and industrial attention because of their low working potential, high capacity, good chemical stability, and eco-friendliness.<span><sup><a href="#anie202423864-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423864-bib-0016_R_d8073168e565" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6">6</a></sup></span> In addition, the available precursors of HCs can be widely sourced from coals,<span><sup><a href="#anie202423864-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423864-bib-0019_R_d8073168e568" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Energy Storage Mater.">7</a></sup></span> biomass,<span><sup><a href="#anie202423864-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423864-bib-0020_R_d8073168e571" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8">8</a></sup></span> and resins.<span><sup><a href="#anie202423864-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423864-bib-0023_R_d8073168e574" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9">9</a></sup></span> Particularly, HCs based on coal resources stands out in virtue of cheap price and high carbon yield, making them promising for practical use in SIBs.<span><sup><a href="#anie202423864-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423864-bib-0026_R_d8073168e577" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10">10</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>Recently, a great number of studies have paid meticulous endeavors to carefully design and fabricate high-performance HCs derived from various coal precursors.<span><sup><a href="#anie202423864-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423864-bib-0029_R_d8073168e582" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11">11</a></sup></span> According to the metamorphic grade, coals can be readily categorized into anthracite,<span><sup><a href="#anie202423864-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423864-bib-0019_R_d8073168e585" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Energy Storage Mater.">7</a>, <a href="#anie202423864-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423864-bib-0030_R_d8073168e585" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11a - Adv. Funct. Mater.">11a</a></sup></span> bituminous coal,<span><sup><a href="#anie202423864-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423864-bib-0032_R_d8073168e588" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12">12</a></sup></span> and liginit.<span><sup><a href="#anie202423864-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423864-bib-0028_R_d8073168e591" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10b - Chem. Eng. J.">10b</a>, <a href="#anie202423864-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423864-bib-0035_R_d8073168e591" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Sci.">13</a></sup></span> It is worth to point out that HCs prepared by direct carbonization of coal precursors suffer low specific capacity, poor initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE), and inferior rate capability, which become daunting hurdles that dramatically impede their commercialization for SIBs.<span><sup><a href="#anie202423864-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423864-bib-0036_R_d8073168e594" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14">14</a></sup></span> This is because coal precursors usually experience ordered arrangement of carbon layers during high temperature pyrolysis, thus forming a highly graphitic and non-porous structure with shallow carbon interlayer spacing that downgrades sodium storage.<span><sup><a href="#anie202423864-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423864-bib-0039_R_d8073168e598" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15">15</a></sup></span> To break this critical limitation, a wealth of chemical modifications have been explored to improve the electrochemical performance of coal-derived HCs.<span><sup><a href="#anie202423864-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423864-bib-0042_R_d8073168e601" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Energ Environ. Mater.">16</a></sup></span> Post-engineering treatment via CO<sub>2</sub>-assisted mechanochemistry was proposed to graft abundant carboxyl groups on HCs for capacity improvement.<span><sup><a href="#anie202423864-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423864-bib-0043_R_d8073168e606" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Electrochim. Acta">17</a></sup></span> Oxidation intercalation method was used to enrich the functional groups of coal precursors for obtaining HCs with enhanced sloping capacity.<span><sup><a href="#anie202423864-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423864-bib-0044_R_d8073168e609" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - J. Energy Storage">18</a></sup></span> However, these HCs exhibit extremely low ICE of &lt;50 %, far beyond the practical requirements. To increase ICE, pre-pore-forming tactic by simple KOH activation could help to obtain closed pores, which remarkably augment the reversible capacity and ICE of HCs.<span><sup><a href="#anie202423864-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423864-bib-0045_R_d8073168e612" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">19</a></sup></span> Moreover, the regulation of crosslinking degree of coal precursors using sucrose,<span><sup><a href="#anie202423864-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423864-bib-0035_R_d8073168e616" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Sci.">13</a>, <a href="#anie202423864-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423864-bib-0046_R_d8073168e616" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Inorg. Chem. Front.">20</a></sup></span> pitch,<span><sup><a href="#anie202423864-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423864-bib-0039_R_d8073168e619" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15">15</a></sup></span> preoxidation<span><sup><a href="#anie202423864-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423864-bib-0038_R_d8073168e622" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14b - Chem. Eng. J.">14b</a>, <a href="#anie202423864-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423864-bib-0047_R_d8073168e622" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Fuel">21</a></sup></span> could inhibit the reordering of carbon layers, thereby constructing plentiful pseudo-graphitic carbon domains for highly efficient sodium storage.<span><sup><a href="#anie202423864-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423864-bib-0048_R_d8073168e625" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Adv. Energy Mater.">22</a></sup></span> This suggests that the precursor modification is of high effectiveness on enabling the enhancement of capacity and ICE. Unfortunately, the reported HCs are subjected to poor reaction kinetics caused by sluggish Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation and pore-filling process, resulting in suboptimal rate performance. Therefore, it is still of utmost urgency to develop new methodology for kinetically promoting the overall sodium storage performance of HCs.</p>
         
         <p>In this study, we propose a powerful strategy to chemically regulate the microstructure of HCs by reconfiguring the functional species of coal precursors. The original hydroxyl and carboxyl groups on the edge/plane terminals of bituminous coal are reconfigured by a simple nitri-oxidation treatment into ester, carbonyl, and nitro functionalities. Such a unique precursor modification effectively suppresses the reordering of carbon layers by steric hindrance effect, thus constructing a desirable pseudo-graphitic structure with expanded carbon interlayer spacing and abundant ultramicropores/closed pores. Additionally, the introduction of oxygen-containing functional groups further creates active sites on the HC surface that are favorable for fast and reversible Na<sup>+</sup> storage. As a consequence, the as-received HCs manifest outstanding sodium storage properties, including a superb specific capacity of 356 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.02 A g<sup>−1</sup>, a notable initial Coulombic efficiency of 88.1 %, excellent rate capability (215 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 2 A g<sup>−1</sup>), and long cycling lifetime over 3000 cycles. The reaction kinetics and sodium storage mechanisms of HCs are also illustrated systematically by a series of advanced characterizations.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202423864-sec-0002">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202423864-sec-0002-title"> Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>It is well-known that the bituminous coal is of moderate metamorphic grade, which is rich in hydroxyl and carboxyl species on the edges/planes of carbon framework.<span><sup><a href="#anie202423864-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423864-bib-0049_R_d8073168e652" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - ACS Appl. Energ. Mater.">23</a></sup></span> These undesirable hydrogen-containing radicals are usually facilitating the reordering of carbon layers during high temperature pyrolysis, behaving like soft carbon.<span><sup><a href="#anie202423864-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423864-bib-0033_R_d8073168e655" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12a - New. Carbon. Mater.">12a</a></sup></span> To address this issue, nitri-oxidation method is proposed to reconfigure the terminal species, as schematically drawn in Figure <a href="#anie202423864-fig-0001">1</a>(a). The nitri-oxidation treatment using concentrated nitric acid could introduce a large amount of ether, ester, carbonyl, and nitro groups into the coal precursor due to the strong oxidation capability of nitric species.<span><sup><a href="#anie202423864-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423864-bib-0050_R_d8073168e661" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24">24</a></sup></span> The abundant hetero N/O-containing species on the edges/planes are prone to retard the movement of aromatic amolecules (i.e., steric hindrance effect), thereby suppressing the ordered restacking of carbon layers.<span><sup><a href="#anie202423864-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423864-bib-0054_R_d8073168e664" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">25</a></sup></span> A disordered carbon structure with expanded interlayer spacing and rich micropores or closed pores is anticipated to be obtained for providing more active sites and fast transport channels toward superb sodium storage.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202423864-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/86007f44-5b07-4e9a-b101-ccabf94235aa/anie202423864-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/86007f44-5b07-4e9a-b101-ccabf94235aa/anie202423864-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/fafbca01-1526-45af-9738-9302df87e629/anie202423864-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/86007f44-5b07-4e9a-b101-ccabf94235aa/anie202423864-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202423864-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202423864" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>(a) The preparation process of coal-derived hard carbons. (b) Peak fitting of XRD patterns. (c) Peak fitting of Raman spectra. (d) FTIR spectra of BC−P and BC−N. (e) C1s spectra peak fitting. (f) O1s spectra peak fitting. (g) N1s spectra peak fitting of precursor.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The crushed coal precursors are composed of blocky granules (Figure S1). X-ray diffraction (XRD) was conducted to identify the microcrystalline features (Figure S2). The broad diffraction peak at around 23° can be fitted into two sub-peaks of aliphatic and aromatic carbons (Figure <a href="#anie202423864-fig-0001">1</a>(b)).<span><sup><a href="#anie202423864-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423864-bib-0035_R_d8073168e695" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Sci.">13</a>, <a href="#anie202423864-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423864-bib-0045_R_d8073168e695" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">19</a>, <a href="#anie202423864-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423864-bib-0055_R_d8073168e695" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Energy Storage Mater.">26</a></sup></span> It is worth to mention that the proportion of aliphatic carbon in precursors increases from 39 % to 64 % after nitri-oxidation treatment, indicating an decrease in aromaticity (Table S1). More structural features of coal precursors were assessed using Raman. Among the fitting curves (Figure <a href="#anie202423864-fig-0001">1</a>(c)), the D1 peak at ~1240 cm<sup>−1</sup> reflects the sp<sup>2</sup>-sp<sup>3</sup> hybridized carbon structure at the graphite edge, while the D2 peak at ~1450 cm<sup>−1</sup> corresponds to the adsorbed molecules, molecular fragments, and oxygen-containing functional groups.<span><sup><a href="#anie202423864-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423864-bib-0044_R_d8073168e710" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - J. Energy Storage">18</a>, <a href="#anie202423864-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423864-bib-0046_R_d8073168e710" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Inorg. Chem. Front.">20</a></sup></span> The intensity ratios of both D1 and D2 peaks relative to the graphitic peak (G) are increased from 0.23/0.24 to 0.45/0.61, respectively, indicating that the nitri-oxidation treatment increases the content of aliphatic carbon and oxygen-containing groups.</p>
         
         <p>Fourier transform infrared spectrometer (FTIR) was used to characterize the functional groups of precursors, as shown in Figure <a href="#anie202423864-fig-0001">1</a>(d). The absorption peaks observed at 2850 and 2925 cm<sup>−1</sup> are associated with the stretching vibrations of −CH<sub>2</sub> and −CH<sub>3</sub> groups.<span><sup><a href="#anie202423864-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423864-bib-0035_R_d8073168e725" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Sci.">13</a>, <a href="#anie202423864-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423864-bib-0046_R_d8073168e725" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Inorg. Chem. Front.">20</a></sup></span> A higher peak intensity for BC−N indicates an increase in aliphatic hydrocarbon content, which is consistent with XRD results. The absorption peaks located at 1715 and 1035 cm<sup>−1</sup> are intensified after nitri-oxidation treatment, suggesting an increase of carbonyl/ester (C=O) and ether (C−O) bonds, respectively. Additionally, the hydroxyl (−OH) peak at 3450 cm<sup>−1</sup> is significantly diminished. Moreover, the emergence of new absorption peaks at 1340 and 1535 cm<sup>−1</sup> corresponds to the symmetric and asymmetric stretching vibrations of nitro groups (−NO<sub>2</sub>), respectively.<span><sup><a href="#anie202423864-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423864-bib-0055_R_d8073168e737" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Energy Storage Mater.">26</a>, <a href="#anie202423864-bib-0056" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423864-bib-0056_R_d8073168e737" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Carbon">27</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>The chemical evolution of surface functionalities on precursors was further revealed by X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS). The stronger intensity of O/N elements (Figure S3) confirms the massive incorporation of N/O species into the precursor by nitri-oxidation, which is consistent with the XRF results (Table S2). From the high-resolution C 1s spectra shown in Figure <a href="#anie202423864-fig-0001">1</a>(e), it is evident that the as-modified precursor (BC−N) is in possession of greater amount of C−O (265.7 eV) and C=O (288.5 eV) components (Table S3). This is in line with the fitting results of O 1s spectrum containing richer C−O (531.3 eV), C=O (532.5 eV), and O−C=O (533.6 eV) species, as present in Figure <a href="#anie202423864-fig-0001">1</a>(f) and Table S4.<span><sup><a href="#anie202423864-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423864-bib-0030_R_d8073168e749" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11a - Adv. Funct. Mater.">11a</a>, <a href="#anie202423864-bib-0057" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423864-bib-0057_R_d8073168e749" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Nano Res.">28</a></sup></span> In addition, the BC−N precursor exhibits a huge peak assigning to oxidized N in the N1s spectrum (Figure <a href="#anie202423864-fig-0001">1</a>(g)), which is an indicator of nitro groups (Table S5). Combined with FTIR analysis, it can be inferred that the nitri-oxidation treatment successfully reconfigures the surface functionalities of bituminous coals into ether, ester, carbonyl, and nitro groups. The abundant O/N-containing chemical species not only restrain the ordered restacking of carbon layers by steric hindrance effect, but also facilitate the incorporation of hetero-atoms into the carbon framework, which will be discussed later.</p>
         
         <p>The difference in thermal behavior of BC−P and BC−N precursors was investigated by thermogravimetric analysis (TGA, Figure S4). The primary weight loss of BC−N occurs at a lower temperature (~250 °C), corresponding to the decomposition of oxygen-containing functional groups, such as ester, carbonyl, and nitro groups. In addition, the total weight loss of BC−N (50 %) is higher than that of BC−P (37 %), corroborating the introduction of a larger quantity of oxygen-containing groups by nitri-oxidation. It is worth to point out that the removal of functional groups during the carbonization process is beneficial for the formation of ultramicropores and closed pores, as will be revealed by the following discussions.</p>
         
         <p>The morphology and microstructure of HCs were imaged by scanning electron microscope (SEM) and transmission electron microscope (TEM). Both samples are comprised of irregular microsized particles, as shown in Figure S5. EDS analysis illustrates the homogeneous distribution of elemental carbon, oxygen and nitrogen throughout the HC−N particle (Figure S6). More detailed microcrystalline structure is meticulously compared from HRTEM images. As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202423864-fig-0002">2</a>(a), HC−P sample exhibits a highly dense and graphitic microstructure with well-ordered arrangement of carbon layers and a narrow interlayer spacing of 0.361 nm. In sharp contrast, HC−N displays a turbostratic structure composed by randomly-stacked curved carbon layers and abundant nanopores/closed pores, as marked by the pink circles (Figure <a href="#anie202423864-fig-0002">2</a>(b)). More importantly, the average spacing of carbon interlayers is greatly expanded to 0.385 nm. This disordered and nanoporous structure is attributed to the nitri-oxidation treatment, which introduces a large amount of N/O-containing functional groups to suppress the reordering of carbon layers and also expand the interlayer spacing via steric hindrance effect. The presence of closed nanopores and enlarged interlayer spacing is of desirable benefit for providing more active sites for sodium-ion storage.<span><sup><a href="#anie202423864-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423864-bib-0045_R_d8073168e766" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">19</a>, <a href="#anie202423864-bib-0058" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423864-bib-0058_R_d8073168e766" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Energy Storage Mater.">29</a></sup></span>
            </p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202423864-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/b74d4e1d-3bf3-40b8-943c-fa1d7f33e0e5/anie202423864-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/b74d4e1d-3bf3-40b8-943c-fa1d7f33e0e5/anie202423864-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/7e228a59-0836-48b7-8ceb-a3c7cbfcb5f5/anie202423864-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/b74d4e1d-3bf3-40b8-943c-fa1d7f33e0e5/anie202423864-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202423864-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202423864" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>HRTEM images and schematic structure of (a) HC−P and (b) HC−N materials. (c) XRD patterns and fitted (002) peaks, (d) Raman spectra, (e) XPS, (f) N<sub>2</sub> adsorption/desorption isotherms, (g) CO<sub>2</sub> adsorption/desorption isotherms, (h) SAXS patterns of HCs. The insets show the corresponding pore size distribution.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>XRD and Raman tests were conducted to further comprehend the microstructure of HCs. Figure S7(a) shows the XRD patterns of HC−N and HC−P samples, where the two broad peaks at around 24° and 43° are allocated to (002) and (100) planes of carbon, respectively. Notably, the (002) peak of HC−N is shifted to a smaller diffraction angle attributed to a larger average interplanar spacing. Generally, the (002) peak can be deconvoluted into disordered carbon (d&gt;0.4 nm), psedudo-graphitic carbon (d=0.36–0.4 nm), and ordered graphite-like carbon (d&lt;0.36 nm) (Figure <a href="#anie202423864-fig-0002">2</a>(c)).<span><sup><a href="#anie202423864-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423864-bib-0025_R_d8073168e802" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9b - Small">9b</a>, <a href="#anie202423864-bib-0059" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423864-bib-0059_R_d8073168e802" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">30</a></sup></span> According to the fitting results (Table S6), the HC−N sample possesses a higher proportion of pseudo-graphitic and disordered structures, again suggesting that the nitro-oxidation treatment can inhibit high-temperature graphitization to obtain a turbostratic structure with expanded interlayer spacing. Raman spectra offer more structural characteristics, as summarized in Table S7. As evidenced in Figure <a href="#anie202423864-fig-0002">2</a>(d), the HCs encompass typical defective carbon induced peak at ~1360 cm<sup>−1</sup> and graphitic carbon-induced peak at ~1610 cm<sup>−1</sup>.</p>
         
         <p>To distinguish more details of carbon structure, the two broad peaks can be finely fitted into four sub-peaks at 1250, 1340, 1475, and 1580 cm<sup>−1</sup>, corresponding to D1, D, D2, and G-bands, respectively. The integrated area ratio of D/G bands (A<sub>D</sub>/A<sub>G</sub>) is increased from 1.68 for HC−N to 0.97 for HC−P, indicating a higher defect concentration in HC−N sample.<span><sup><a href="#anie202423864-bib-0060" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423864-bib-0060_R_d8073168e821" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31">31</a></sup></span> The XRD and Raman results are in good accordance with the disordered structure of HC−N observed from HRTEM (Figure <a href="#anie202423864-fig-0002">2</a>(b)).</p>
         
         <p>The chemical composition and their electronic bonding states of HCs were studied by XPS. The survey XPS (Figure S7(b)) shows the existence of C, O, and N species in HC−N. The C 1s spectrum is deconvoluted as C−C sp<sup>2</sup>, C−C sp<sup>3</sup>, C−O and C=O species.<span><sup><a href="#anie202423864-bib-0063" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423864-bib-0063_R_d8073168e835" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32">32</a></sup></span> The higher content of sp<sup>3</sup> C−C suggests a more disordered carbon structure for the HC−N material (Figure S8(a)). Similarly, the O1s peak can be resolved into three chemical components of C−O, C=O, and O=C−O species (Figure <a href="#anie202423864-fig-0002">2</a>(e)).<span><sup><a href="#anie202423864-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423864-bib-0041_R_d8073168e844" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15b - Energy Storage Mater.">15b</a></sup></span> It is notable that HC−N is more rich in oxygen-containing species, and the oxygen content attains to 5.68 % (vs. 3.34 % for HC−P). Moreover, compared to HC−P, HC−N contains a certain amount of N species (1.13 %) due to the incorporation of nitro groups during nitri-oxidation treatment (Figure S8(b) and S9). The documented studies establish that the chemical functionalities of ester, carbonyl, and nitrogen species are highly conducive for reversible surface Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption and rapid transportation of Na<sup>+</sup> ions.<span><sup><a href="#anie202423864-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423864-bib-0042_R_d8073168e851" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Energ Environ. Mater.">16</a>, <a href="#anie202423864-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423864-bib-0055_R_d8073168e851" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Energy Storage Mater.">26</a></sup></span> Accordingly, it is easy to infer that the higher proportion of these favorable functionalities for HC−N could impose positive effect to advance sodium storage capability.</p>
         
         <p>The porous structure of HCs was then investigated using both N<sub>2</sub> and CO<sub>2</sub> adsorption/desorption measurements. Table S8 summarizes the porous parameters. Figure <a href="#anie202423864-fig-0002">2</a>(f) shows the N<sub>2</sub> isotherm and the corresponding pore size distribution. HC−N sample presents a large Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) specific surface area of 124.5 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>, in steep contrast to only 3.4 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup> for HC−P. Additionally, a plenty of mesopores (2–4 nm) are noted for HC−N material, which is beneficial for electrolyte ingress and diffusion. The microporous texture is further illustrated by CO<sub>2</sub> isotherms.<span><sup><a href="#anie202423864-bib-0066" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423864-bib-0066_R_d8073168e877" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33">33</a></sup></span> As plotted in Figure <a href="#anie202423864-fig-0002">2</a>(g), the HC−N not only shows a significant increase in the adsorption volume, but also possesses a substantial quantity of ultra-micropores below 1 nm. The internal pore structure of HCs was scrutinized by small-angle X-ray scattering (SAXS) measurements.<span><sup><a href="#anie202423864-bib-0069" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423864-bib-0069_R_d8073168e884" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34">34</a></sup></span> As depicted in Figure <a href="#anie202423864-fig-0002">2</a>(h), HC−N exhibits a more broad hump with higher intensity in the scattering vector range of 0.1 to 0.4 Å<sup>−1</sup>, indicative of a greater number of closed-pore structures as well as an increased volume of these closed pores. Figure S10 displays a meticulous fitting of the SAXS data for HC−N, clearly identifying the closed pores with a mean size of 1.37 nm. These findings collectively indicate that the nitri-oxidation treatment reconfigures the terminal species of precursor to promote the formation of ultramicropores/closed pores during carbonization, which could serve sufficient active sites for Na<sup>+</sup> filling.</p>
         
         <p>The Na<sup>+</sup> storage properties of HC anodes was then assessed by various electrochemical techniques. Both the HC−N and HC−P samples present similar cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves, as compared between Figure <a href="#anie202423864-fig-0003">3</a>(a) and Figure S11, indicating identical Na<sup>+</sup> storage behavior. Specifically, the relatively weak and broad cathodic peak at approximately 0.6 V observed in the first cycle is caused by the formation of solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) film accompanying with the electrolyte decomposition and Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption/trapping on the surface/defects sites of anode.<span><sup><a href="#anie202423864-bib-0072" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423864-bib-0072_R_d8073168e906" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Adv. Energy Mater.">35</a></sup></span> The following strong redox peak at around 0.1 V is in close association with the highly reversible insertion/extraction and pore filling/removal of Na<sup>+</sup> ions within the carbon framework.<span><sup><a href="#anie202423864-bib-0073" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423864-bib-0073_R_d8073168e912" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36">36</a></sup></span> The overlapping of CV curves in the subsequent cycles signifies good reaction reversibility of the HC anodes. The Na<sup>+</sup> storage behavior is also confirmed by galvanostatic charge/discharge technique. Furthermore, the depth-resolved XPS analysis at different sputtering depths (0, 10, 20 nm) reveals the chemical evolution of the SEI layer. As depicted in Figure S12, the intensity of both C−F and NaF peaks changes with depth, demonstrating the distinct layer composition of the SEI formed on the electrodes. The presence of a NaF-rich SEI layer on HC−N, as compared to the HC−P counterpart, is of advanced benefit for superior electrochemical performance by stabilizing the interface and facilitating the Na<sup>+</sup> ion transport. This evidence supports the relationship between the SEI composition, the pore structure, and the electrochemical behavior of the electrodes.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202423864-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/59d3be6e-886e-4866-b1cf-fadbc45974a7/anie202423864-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/59d3be6e-886e-4866-b1cf-fadbc45974a7/anie202423864-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/4641052d-09e7-4c01-a6fc-09a9531b1e31/anie202423864-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/59d3be6e-886e-4866-b1cf-fadbc45974a7/anie202423864-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202423864-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202423864" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>(a) CV curves of HC−N. (b) Galvanostatic discharge/charge profiles at 0.02 A g<sup>−1</sup>. (c) A comparison of slope/plateau capacity. (d) Cycling (0.05 A g<sup>−1</sup>) and (e) rate performance of HC−N and HC−P anodes. (f) Comparison of sodium storage properties of our HC−N sample with coal-based HCs reported in previous studies. (g) Long-term cycling stability at 1 A g<sup>−1</sup>.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>To determine the optimal carbonization temperature, the electrochemical performance of samples prepared at various carbonization temperatures was evaluated. As shown in Figure S13–S16, the samples carbonized at 1300 °C exhibit the best electrochemical performance, which are used for comprehensive investigation thereafter. As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202423864-fig-0003">3</a>(b), the discharge profiles of both anodes are composed of one sloped segment within 0.1–2 V potential range and one long plateau region below 0.1 V, which is consistent with CV analysis. More remarkably, the HC−N sample could output a reversible capacity of 356 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.02 A g<sup>−1</sup> along with a high ICE of 88.1 %, both of which surpass the HC−P counterparts (266 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and 83.5 %). The capacity loss in the first cycle is resulted from the formation of SEI film and some Na<sup>+</sup> capturing by defects and narrow interlayers.<span><sup><a href="#anie202423864-bib-0076" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423864-bib-0076_R_d8073168e963" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Nano Energy">37</a></sup></span> By separating the capacity contributed by plateau/sloping regions (Figure <a href="#anie202423864-fig-0003">3</a>(c)), the HC−N electrode shows a significant enhancement in both sloping and plateau capacities. This can be well explained by the unique structural features of HC−N containing expanded cabon interlayer spacing, abundant ultra-micropores/closed pores, and increased surface functionalities.<span><sup><a href="#anie202423864-bib-0077" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423864-bib-0077_R_d8073168e969" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Nano Energy">38</a>, <a href="#anie202423864-bib-0078" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423864-bib-0078_R_d8073168e969" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Adv. Energy Mater.">39</a></sup></span> The cycling performance was measured at 0.05 A g<sup>−1</sup>. As depicted in Figure <a href="#anie202423864-fig-0003">3</a>(d), the HC−N sample preserves a high specific capacity of 350 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 110 cycles, revealing its excellent cycling endurance.</p>
         
         <p>Rate capability is an important metric for HCs to be used in SIBs. Figure <a href="#anie202423864-fig-0003">3</a>(e) presents the rate performance of HC anodes. It is noteworthy that the HC−N anode exhibits a markedly enhanced rate performance compared to the HC−P anode, showcasing its better rate capability. Even when the current density increases to as large as 2.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>, the HC−N could retains a high capacity of 215 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. In addition, the capacity could fully restore its initial levels upon switching backward to 0.02 A g<sup>−1</sup>, substantiating the dynamic sodiation/desodiation stability and reversibility at different current rates. The charge and discharge profiles at various current densities are presented in Figure S14(b) and Figure S16(b). Such an outstanding sodium storage performance (both high specific capacity and high ICE) stands out among the coal-based HC anodes reported in the literature, as compared in Figure <a href="#anie202423864-fig-0003">3</a>(f) and Table S9.<span><sup><a href="#anie202423864-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423864-bib-0028_R_d8073168e995" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10b - Chem. Eng. J.">10b</a>, <a href="#anie202423864-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423864-bib-0031_R_d8073168e995" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11b - Carbon">11b</a>, <a href="#anie202423864-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423864-bib-0035_R_d8073168e995" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Sci.">13</a>, <a href="#anie202423864-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423864-bib-0038_R_d8073168e995" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14b - Chem. Eng. J.">14b</a>, <a href="#anie202423864-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423864-bib-0039_R_d8073168e995" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15">15</a>, <a href="#anie202423864-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423864-bib-0042_R_d8073168e995" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Energ Environ. Mater.">16</a>, <a href="#anie202423864-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423864-bib-0044_R_d8073168e995" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - J. Energy Storage">18</a>, <a href="#anie202423864-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423864-bib-0045_R_d8073168e995" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">19</a>, <a href="#anie202423864-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423864-bib-0055_R_d8073168e995" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Energy Storage Mater.">26</a>, <a href="#anie202423864-bib-0065" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423864-bib-0065_R_d8073168e995" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32b - New. Carbon. Mater.">32b</a></sup></span> Long-term cycling performance of HC−N anode was further evaluated at 1.0 A g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure S17). As depicted in Figure <a href="#anie202423864-fig-0003">3</a>(g), an excellent capacity retention of 91 % is achieved with a Coulombic efficiency close to 100 % after 3000 discharge/charge cycles, again elucidating the robust electrode stability. The electrochemical performance of HC−N was further evaluated in ester-based electrolytes, as illustrated in Figure S18. The results demonstrate that HC−N also exhibits high specific capacity (339.2 mAh/g) and ICE (81.5 %) in ester-based electrolytes, highlighting its versatility and potential for a broader range of applications.</p>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element">The sodium-ion storage kinetics of HC electrodes was elucidated by electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS). As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202423864-fig-0004">4</a>(a), the resulted Nyquist plots consist of a depressed semicircle at the high-frequency region and a straight line at the low-frequency region.<span><sup><a href="#anie202423864-bib-0067" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423864-bib-0067_R_d8073168e1010" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33a - Adv. Energy Mater.">33a</a></sup></span> The data were fitted using the equivalent circuit model shown in the Figure, revealing that HC−N exhibits a significantly lower charge-transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>, 12.2 Ω) compared to HC−P (21.2 Ω). Moreover, the apparent Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient (<span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/59dc1925-9594-45a2-aadc-d88d75ec80c4/anie202423864-math-0001.png"></span><math></math>
, cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>) can be further calculated according to the following Equation (Eq <a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#anie202423864-disp-0001" title="Link to equation">1</a>):<span><sup><a href="#anie202423864-bib-0079" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423864-bib-0079_R_d8073168e1043" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Carbon">40</a></sup></span><div class="inline-equation" id="anie202423864-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                  
                  <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/06f35ecd-32b3-43b3-a097-aae0ecc728cd/anie202423864-math-0002.png"></span><math></math>
                  </span><span class="inline-equation__label">(1)</span></div>
            </div>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202423864-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/3686de4a-2b52-4361-88fe-617402e4028b/anie202423864-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/3686de4a-2b52-4361-88fe-617402e4028b/anie202423864-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/2e13bc87-304f-40ad-8260-1d4b837c1a65/anie202423864-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/3686de4a-2b52-4361-88fe-617402e4028b/anie202423864-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202423864-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202423864" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>(a) Nyquist plots of HC and HC−N electrodes (inset shows the equivalent circuit model). (b) CV curves at different rates of HC−N. (c) Fitting lines between peak current and scan rate. (d) <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/db899f4c-bd84-4880-b641-2eef4aef0f94/anie202423864-math-0003.png"></span><math></math>
 values for the anodic and cathodic peaks. (e) <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/fa07b8ec-2a8e-4b45-9be9-7e8a46615752/anie202423864-math-0004.png"></span><math></math>
 values calculated from GITT profiles. (f) In situ Raman spectra of HC−N electrode and its corresponding 2D counter plot of peak intensity.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>where, the parameters of <i>R</i>, T, A, <i>n</i>, F, and C refers to the gas constant, the absolute temperature (298.15 K herein), the surface area of electrode, the electron transfer number per unit active material, the Faraday constant, and the Na<sup>+</sup> concentration. The Warburg factor (<i>σ</i>) is determined by the slope of Z′ versus ω<sup>−1/2</sup>. According to the slope values of HC−P and HC−N electrodes plotted in Figure S19 (a), the apparent <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/90f9bc10-138c-43de-9ebe-038a9def029d/anie202423864-math-0005.png"></span><math></math>
 value of HC−N electrode (1.04×10<sup>−10</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>) is about tenfold of the control electrode (1.17×10<sup>−11</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>). The combination of low <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> and high <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/c30dcd60-c517-4fa5-8c71-ffb4a11762b3/anie202423864-math-0006.png"></span><math></math>
 of HC−N electrode jointly contributes to fast reaction kinetics that is responsible for its better rate performance.</p>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element">The reaction kinetics was further studied by CV conducted at various scan rates from 0.2 to 1 mV s<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a href="#anie202423864-fig-0004">4</a>(b) and Figure S19 (b)). The shape of redox peaks is well-retained at high scan rate, indicating swift Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction processes. The relationship between peak current (<i>i<sub>p</sub></i>) and scan rate follows the Randles-sevcik equation (Eq <a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#anie202423864-disp-0002" title="Link to equation">2</a>):<span><sup><a href="#anie202423864-bib-0080" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423864-bib-0080_R_d8073168e1241" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Susmat">41</a></sup></span><div class="inline-equation" id="anie202423864-disp-0002"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                  
                  <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/40ce53e1-4fc3-4b3b-b34e-eae0c6f73e3b/anie202423864-math-0007.png"></span><math></math>
                  </span><span class="inline-equation__label">(2)</span></div>
            </div>
         
         <p>As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202423864-fig-0004">4</a>(c), the linear relationship for both cathodic and anodic peaks reveals a diffusion-controlled reaction. The Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient is calculated based on the above equation. It is notable that the HC−N electrode exhibit higher <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/eb0f2b8e-6517-407a-be2a-993687980010/anie202423864-math-0008.png"></span><math></math>
 values than that of the HC−P (Figure <a href="#anie202423864-fig-0004">4</a>(d)), agreeing well with the EIS result.</p>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element">An-depth understanding of reaction kinetics under different charge/discharge potential states is probed by galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT, Figure S20). The corresponding <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/45e5fe97-b10f-4d7d-83e8-ee00e37fc254/anie202423864-math-0009.png"></span><math></math>
 can be acquired from Fick's second law (Eq. <a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#anie202423864-disp-0003" title="Link to equation">3</a>):<span><sup><a href="#anie202423864-bib-0081" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423864-bib-0081_R_d8073168e1348" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Carbon">42</a></sup></span><div class="inline-equation" id="anie202423864-disp-0003"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                  
                  <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/70c5fe11-6b1c-4860-b575-26786d373df2/anie202423864-math-0010.png"></span><math></math>
                  </span><span class="inline-equation__label">(3)</span></div>
            </div>
         
         <p>Here, the parameters of <i>τ</i>, <i>m<sub>B</sub></i>, <i>M<sub>B</sub></i>, <i>V<sub>M</sub></i>, <i>S</i>, <i>ΔE</i><sub><i>τ</i></sub>, and <i>ΔE<sub>s</sub></i> represent the pulse duration, the mass and molar ratio of carbon, thelectrode surface area, the molar volume of carbon, the instantaneous voltage change under a constant current, and the voltage difference induced by a pulse current, respectively. Figure <a href="#anie202423864-fig-0004">4</a>(e) depicts the resulted <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/c10e3c36-6e7b-4675-955f-26973f55fe2b/anie202423864-math-0011.png"></span><math></math>
 values under various applied potentials. The HC electrodes display an elevated <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/a7042ca6-0099-4f5c-ab80-5c5395bf77c6/anie202423864-math-0012.png"></span><math></math>
 in the high-potential sloping region associated with fast Na<sup>+</sup> surface adsorption process and a rapid drop of <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/71873b07-b181-4b0a-b493-743abeebc0ae/anie202423864-math-0013.png"></span><math></math>
 in the low-potential plateau region due to the sluggish diffusion-controlled Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation and/or pore-filling process. The abrupt rise of <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/65e21f9a-61c5-4bb2-ba10-23dfbb7a9131/anie202423864-math-0014.png"></span><math></math>
 at the end of discharge is related to the easy entrance of Na<sup>+</sup> into the outsides of carbon interlayers.<span><sup><a href="#anie202423864-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423864-bib-0055_R_d8073168e1519" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Energy Storage Mater.">26</a></sup></span> It is important to note that the HC−N electrode exhibits much higher <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/286dadcc-55bf-4270-bc79-9208b3985a69/anie202423864-math-0015.png"></span><math></math>
 than the control ones over the whole charge/discharge processes, which can be readily attributed to the expanded interlayer spacing of carbon layers, the highly nanoporous structure, as well as the rich oxygen-containing functional groups on the surface.</p>
         
         <p>The relationship between nitrogen/oxygen functional groups and sodium storage behavior is elucidated by XPS analysis. Figure S21 presents the high-resolution XPS spectra of HC−N electrodes at different charge states (2 V and 0.01 V), revealing the evolution of active sites during the electrochemical process. The C1s spectra show a decrease in carbonyl (C=O) groups and an increase in ether (C−O) groups upon discharge, demonstrating their role in sodium ion adsorption. The O1s and N1s spectra further highlight the dynamic behavior of oxygen-containing groups and nitrogen functionalities (Figure S22). These results confirm the critical role of nitrogen and oxygen functional groups in facilitating sodium storage and enhancing the electrochemical performance of HC−N.</p>
         
         <p>In situ Raman spectroscopy was performed on the HC−N anode to reveal the sodium-ion storage mechanism. As evidenced from Figure <a href="#anie202423864-fig-0004">4</a>(f), the discharge process does not alter the peak position of D-bands, but the peak intensity is gradually weakened, resulting in the rapid decline of I<sub>D</sub>/I<sub>G</sub>, which is ascribed to the surface adsorption and occupation of defect sites by Na<sup>+</sup> (Figure S23).<span><sup><a href="#anie202423864-bib-0082" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423864-bib-0082_R_d8073168e1554" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43">43</a></sup></span> As for the G band peak, in addition to the reduced peak intensity, there is a significant redshift in the plateau stage. This phenomenon is due to the intercalation of Na<sup>+</sup> into graphene interlayer weakens and elongates the C−C bonds that leads to the electron occupation in π* antibonding orbitals.<span><sup><a href="#anie202423864-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423864-bib-0030_R_d8073168e1560" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11a - Adv. Funct. Mater.">11a</a>, <a href="#anie202423864-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423864-bib-0055_R_d8073168e1560" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Energy Storage Mater.">26</a></sup></span> Upon the reverse charging, both the D and G bands return to their initial states, indicating that the Na<sup>+</sup> storage behavior of HC−N is highly reversible.</p>
         
         <p>To confirm the Na states at different potentials, the HC electrodes discharged to 0.3 V (slope region) and 0.01 V (plateau region) were disassembled and soaked in a solution of phenolphthalein/ethanol (Figure S24). Notably, the solution remains transparent color in the slope region, indicating surface adsorption of Na<sup>+</sup>. When it comes to the plateau region, the solution color evolves to purple, a typical NaOH-induced phenomenon due to the reaction between metallic Na with ethanol, verifying the presence of metallic Na clusters filled in the nanopores.<span><sup><a href="#anie202423864-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423864-bib-0003_R_d8073168e1570" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1b - ACS Energy Lett.">1b</a>, <a href="#anie202423864-bib-0080" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423864-bib-0080_R_d8073168e1570" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Susmat">41</a>, <a href="#anie202423864-bib-0085" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423864-bib-0085_R_d8073168e1570" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44">44</a></sup></span> Moreover, the solution color of HC−N anode is more darker than that of HC−P, suggesting that the HC−N affords much richer nanopores for more storage of metallic Na clusters. Depth-resolved XPS analysis of Na 1s spectra provides further evidence for this phenomenon. Through depth-resolved XPS analysis, the influence of SEI-derived sodium on the Na 1s signal was effectively eliminated (Figure S25). The comparison of Na 1s spectra at different potentials reveals a distinct shift toward higher binding energy at 0.01 V compared to 2 V (Figure S26). This shift confirms the formation of sodium metal clusters during deep discharge. It can be convincingly inferred that the sodium-ion storage mechanism of HC−N follows surface adsorption, Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation into carbon interlayers, and Na<sup>+</sup> filling into ultra-micropores/closed pores.</p>
         
         <p>The practical potential of high-performance HC−N materials is further demonstrated by assembling full cells with commercial NVP cathodes (Figure <a href="#anie202423864-fig-0005">5</a>(a)). As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202423864-fig-0005">5</a>(b), the NVP could deliver a high operating plateau of 3.4 V and a good capacity of 110 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, which make it suitable for coupling with the HC−N anodes. The resulting charge/discharge profiles of full cell is displayed in Figure <a href="#anie202423864-fig-0005">5</a>(c). It is noticeable that the cell could achieve an average working voltage of 3.3 V and a high capacity of 323 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> based on anode mass at 0.05 A g<sup>−1</sup> accompanying with decent cycling stability (Figure <a href="#anie202423864-fig-0005">5</a>(d)). More remarkably, the narrow voltage gap in between charge/discharge profiles indicates small polarization, leading to a well-preservation of plateaus at high current rates (Figure S27). The corresponding rate performance is shown in Figure <a href="#anie202423864-fig-0005">5e</a>. Impressively, the full cell retains a capacity of 149 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> when the current rate increases to as high as 5 A g<sup>−1</sup>, indicating excellent rate performance. To mimic the practicality in real-life circumstances, the full cell was used to power a LED light belt. As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202423864-fig-0005">5e</a>, the successful working state demonstrates the potential applications of HC−N sample for various electronics.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202423864-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/d16dddee-8d5e-41f8-8caf-9eef2e7949f1/anie202423864-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/d16dddee-8d5e-41f8-8caf-9eef2e7949f1/anie202423864-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/bba6ff93-601e-44aa-92cb-0bfce78d01b8/anie202423864-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/d16dddee-8d5e-41f8-8caf-9eef2e7949f1/anie202423864-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">

                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>(a) Scheme of NVP<span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/59541cd9-96f6-4511-9646-1f135575d45b/anie202423864-math-0016.png"></span><math></math>

         </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202423864-sec-0003">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202423864-sec-0003-title"> Conclusions</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, the microstructure of coal-based HCs has been successfully and finely regulated by reconfiguring the surface functionalities of precursors. Nitri-oxidation treatment is of great effectiveness for incorporating ether, ester, carbonyl, and nitro species, which afford steric hindrance to inhibit the ordered rearrangement of carbon layers. Remarkably, the as-constructed HC−N features unique pseudo-graphitic structure with expanded carbon interlayer spacing, and plentiful ultramicropores/closed pores, and rich O/N-functionalities. Benefiting from the structural merits, a high specific capacity of 356 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> is obtained at 0.02 A g<sup>−1</sup> along with an improved initial Coulombic efficiency of 88.1 %, excellent rate capability, and long cycling lifespan. Electrochemical characterizations and in situ Raman analysis substantiate the working mechanism of surface adsorption and interlayer intercalation/pore filling of Na<sup>+</sup>. The present study furnishes a powerful design strategy of chemical reconfiguration for accelerating the practical deployment of sodium-ion batteries by advancing high-performance hard carbons beyond coal precursors.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
